    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5226863831515935966">De familie van Aga Djan is al eeuwenlang de belangrijkste familie in de Iraanse stad Senedjan. Zij wonen in het huis naast de Djomè-moskee, de grootste moskee van een stad waar het vrijdaggebed wordt gehouden.   <span id="freeText5226863831515935966" style="display:none">De familie van Aga Djan is al eeuwenlang de belangrijkste familie in de Iraanse stad Senedjan. Zij wonen in het huis naast de Djomè-moskee, de grootste moskee van een stad waar het vrijdaggebed wordt gehouden. Van generatie op generatie komt de imam van de moskee uit de familie van Aga Djan; als het verhaal opent, is dit zijn neef Alsaberi. Als tapijtverkoper staat Aga Djan bovendien aan het hoofd van de bazaar en heeft economische macht. Kader Abdolah vertelt in deze roman over de geschiedenis van Iran door in te zoomen op de invloed van de gebeurtenissen op deze familie. Als de revolutie wordt voorbereid en uitbreekt, komt de samenleving onder druk te staan en verliest de familie langzaamaan al hun invloed en zekerheden.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5226863831515935966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText1700821437928464269" style="display:none">I liked you a lot, Aqa Jaan.<br />You were there when it all began, and you trusted that everything would turn out well for all your relatives.<br />Little did you know how much your world would change, and most of your countrymen were as clueless as you. <br />From growing political dissatisfaction to political turmoil in only ten years and from one strict ruler to another, only the head dress were different. <br />You remind me of people I met in Iran. Some who thought that after all the change were for the better and many who dreamt of a way out.<br />You remind me of an old woman I met on the street, all in black and she seemed so far away from everything I knew.<br />As I passed her in the street, she turned to me, just a little, smiled from under the chador and whispered in broken English; Welcome to Iran.<br />This was what The House of the Mosque did to me.<br />Re-welcomed me to Iran and reminded me once again that we are not so different from each other after all. <br /><br />Enjoy the story, and refrain from too much fact checking - Kader Abdolah has taken himself a few liberties. <br />Is it a political statement? Certainly, but also a loveletter to the last 3-4 generations, the ones who saw growth and wealth under the Shah, the ones who were repressed and the ones who chose a new captain. The young generation will find ways of balancing their history and come to terms with it, and they already are.</span>

<div id="review_135982826" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/135982826" />
    <a title="Joe Strong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4688080-joe-strong"><img alt="Joe Strong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364642633p2/4688080.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/135982826?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Strong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Strong" href="/user/show/4688080-joe-strong">Joe Strong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847672414" href="/book/show/9280831-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135982826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9536265084923296319">I have used the word unique a few times to describe a novel, perhaps I did not know the true meaning of the word. Or perhaps, it was that I had not read The House of the Mosque, nor will I ever read a book of the same calibre, style, or story, I can't imagine.<br/>At first I was sceptical, as any boy would be after being recommended a book by his father, who, frankly, has bad taste. But then I had nothing to read, and this had a vibrant cover...<br/>Immediately I was absolutely absorbed. I was almost surp</span>
  <span id="freeText9536265084923296319" style="display:none">I have used the word unique a few times to describe a novel, perhaps I did not know the true meaning of the word. Or perhaps, it was that I had not read The House of the Mosque, nor will I ever read a book of the same calibre, style, or story, I can't imagine.<br />At first I was sceptical, as any boy would be after being recommended a book by his father, who, frankly, has bad taste. But then I had nothing to read, and this had a vibrant cover...<br />Immediately I was absolutely absorbed. I was almost surprised; Abdolah does not use long descriptions akin to Woolf or any Russian author ever. Instead, he respects our imagination, he allows us as the readers a bit of breathing space to help create the world.<br />Use of the word ‘create’ is wrong. The book smacks of autobiography, making the story all the more moving. Abdolah (a pen name used in memory of two of his friends killed by the Iranian regime) uses a strangely simple writing style, in fact it is probably the simplest style I have come across. It could be plausible that this is due to the fact than it is an Iranian writing in Dutch that was translated to English. I disagree. The book did not need the complex language and descriptions akin to Woolf or every Russian to have ever written. If I’m honest, I would never have liked it so much had it been some complex metaphor. Instead, Abdolah allows the story to reign, and it took over my mind in a way I never thought possible.<br />A beautiful story, written in a beautiful way, that follows the life a family who live in and run the biggest Mosque in Senejan, a large Iranian city. They live through dictatorship, revolution, and the worse dictatorship after. The book starkly shows us the true Iran, the sincerity of the book is astounding, there was no emotion from the author, in fact it felt like there was no author at all, but instead that I was just watching something, or reading a biography (which I suppose I was). <br />It dispels myths, it attacks fanatics, and it attacks humanity; the horribly fickle gene we as humans all possess. I think from now on I shall carry it in my bag, so the next time someone says an anti-Muslim comment I can throw the book at them to read. Also, I reckon it could do some damage. <br />The ending was the best ending I have ever read. There is no pretence, some have claimed it is abrupt, I might agree. But it meant for me the mystery lived on, and their lives. This is writing at is best; at its core. Damn you, Goodreads.com. Why don’t we have a secret 6 star option?!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9536265084923296319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who liked the book Persepolis and who are interested in Iranian history</span></div>

      Middle East North Africa Lit group

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4656570-jalilah?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4656570-jalilah?shelf=middle-east-north-africa">middle-east-north-africa</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer858309344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14918930220941796626">The House of the Mosque was both deeply moving and informative. The many Iranians I have known have all been very secular. It was very interesting reading about the changes in Iran from the perspective of a conservative family, where the woman all wore chadors, had no television in the house and believed going to the cinema was sinful. Although they were so different from my own experiences and beliefs, I ended up both really liking and sympathizing with Aga Jahn and his family. The pacing was g</span>
  <span id="freeText14918930220941796626" style="display:none">The House of the Mosque was both deeply moving and informative. The many Iranians I have known have all been very secular. It was very interesting reading about the changes in Iran from the perspective of a conservative family, where the woman all wore chadors, had no television in the house and believed going to the cinema was sinful. Although they were so different from my own experiences and beliefs, I ended up both really liking and sympathizing with Aga Jahn and his family. The pacing was good, for I never felt the book was too slow and I really enjoyed all the Magical Realistic elements, like the role of all the different birds in the story. My only complaint would be the men's characters were so much more multidimensional than the female characters. As the Iranian revolution under Ayatollah Khomeini's rule progresses, the story becomes heartbreaking, but the ending is hopeful. All in all, it is a wonderful book.</span>

<div id="review_2031993155" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=islam">islam</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2031993155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8589204378996716407">This book begins calmly as a story about a family in a religious city in Iran. Abdolah zooms in on the figure of Aga Djan, who thoughtfully manages both his (larger) family, the mosque and the bazar of his town and is respected by every one. This specific, rather exotic world is outlined in a very sympathetic, sometimes touching way with great attention for human details. But gradually things change: modernity penetrates the country, as a conscious result of the policies of the Shah and his Amer</span>
  <span id="freeText8589204378996716407" style="display:none">This book begins calmly as a story about a family in a religious city in Iran. Abdolah zooms in on the figure of Aga Djan, who thoughtfully manages both his (larger) family, the mosque and the bazar of his town and is respected by every one. This specific, rather exotic world is outlined in a very sympathetic, sometimes touching way with great attention for human details. But gradually things change: modernity penetrates the country, as a conscious result of the policies of the Shah and his American allies; the resistance against this policy grows stronger, through the imams and the left opposition. Abdolah outlines these developments both in a documentary way, and through the eyes of Aga Djan. And then things go fast: the revolution breaks out, the Shah flees, ayatollah Khomeini takes over power and establishes a reign of terror. Abdolah transponds all this in the dramatic events this causes in the life of Aga Djan and his family. <br /><br />This book is a bliss to read, it’s a handsomely told story and as far as I'm concerned a much better introduction to what happened in Iran than the so acclaimed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9516.Persepolis_The_Story_of_a_Childhood__Persepolis___1_" title="Persepolis The Story of a Childhood (Persepolis, #1) by Marjane Satrapi" rel="nofollow noopener">Persepolis: The Story of a Childhood</a> by Satrapi. Of course this is a 'coloured' report of the Iranian Revolution and some aspects of it are rather dubious, but from all pages of this books speaks the strong love of Abdolah for his homeland.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8589204378996716407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_216928610" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216928610" />
    <a title="James Souttar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6495760-james-souttar"><img alt="James Souttar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317331960p2/6495760.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216928610?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Souttar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Souttar" href="/user/show/6495760-james-souttar">James Souttar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847672407" href="/book/show/6688712-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216928610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3957650538937478441">I ran out of reading material while we were in Italy, and found this in a bookshop that had a collection of English books. I wasn't sure what to make of it to start with: it was written in a very simple language (which may or may not reflect the Dutch original - I don't know) which reminded me of Paulo Coelho's early work (e.g. The Alchemist). But as the story gathered pace, I found myself drawn further and further into it. <br/><br/>The book revolves around the household of Aqa Jan, respected leader of a</span>
  <span id="freeText3957650538937478441" style="display:none">I ran out of reading material while we were in Italy, and found this in a bookshop that had a collection of English books. I wasn't sure what to make of it to start with: it was written in a very simple language (which may or may not reflect the Dutch original - I don't know) which reminded me of Paulo Coelho's early work (e.g. The Alchemist). But as the story gathered pace, I found myself drawn further and further into it. <br /><br />The book revolves around the household of Aqa Jan, respected leader of a small Iranian city's business community and hereditary custodian of the city's Friday Mosque. Beginning in the early 1960s, it tells the story of Iran through the lens of this one family - following the modernization of the Shah's regime, and its unpopularity to the largely conservative people outside of the capital, through to the Iranian revolution, and its aftermath.<br /><br />But really this book is more than a straightforward historical narrative, and this is where it really engaged me. It is a portrait of a model of masculinity, represented by the principal character, Aqa Jan, that we have lost in our societies. Aqa Jan is wise, responsible, caring, spiritually oriented but also a successful entrepreneur - but also very human. The terrible events the family lives through robs him of his son, his respect, his influence, the mosque he has devoted his life to. But it doesn't rob him of his dignity, or his humanity. And it is pretty clear from the author's end note that he is based on a real person, and not simply a literary creation. <br /><br />I loved this book and heartily recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3957650538937478441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2496405379" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2496405379" />
    <a title="Anthony Ferner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45348446-anthony-ferner"><img alt="Anthony Ferner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519908613p2/45348446.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2496405379?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anthony Ferner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anthony Ferner" href="/user/show/45348446-anthony-ferner">Anthony Ferner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45348446-anthony-ferner?shelf=dutch-fiction">dutch-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2496405379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2727298304792953876">This highly readable and engaging semi-autobiographical novel (the author fled Iran for The Netherlands in 1985) tells the story of the Iranian revolution, from the late 1960s onwards. Events are seen from the perspective of one family in the provincial town of Senejan, not far from the religious city of Qom. The attractive central character is Aga Djaan, whose compound includes the mosque - Het huis van de moskee of the title. There he lives with his extended family, including his cousin the mu</span>
  <span id="freeText2727298304792953876" style="display:none">This highly readable and engaging semi-autobiographical novel (the author fled Iran for The Netherlands in 1985) tells the story of the Iranian revolution, from the late 1960s onwards. Events are seen from the perspective of one family in the provincial town of Senejan, not far from the religious city of Qom. The attractive central character is Aga Djaan, whose compound includes the mosque - Het huis van de moskee of the title. There he lives with his extended family, including his cousin the muezzin, and the family of the imam - whose role has been handed down from father to son for generations. Aga Djaan is influential in the bazaar, has close contacts with the powers-that-be. He is a carpet manufacturer and trader, a respected, thoughtful and generous local figure, practitioner of a humane and benevolent (albeit patriarchical) strand of Islam. But that is about to change as opposition grows to the westernising regime of the Shah. The return and accession to power of Ayatollah Khomeini in 1979 unleashes a terrifying wave of religious fervour, bigotry and violence. A son-in-law of Aga Djaan, who has abandoned his wife, becomes a key figure in the Revolution: a pitiless Islamic judge who hands out death sentences with abandon. The cruelty is compounded by the long war with Iraq. The turbulence brings unwelcome changes to the traditional rhythms and relationships in the house of the mosque. Profound tragedy and grief strike Aga Djaan’s household, as some members embrace religious extremism, others get involved in leftist opposition. The mood is lightened towards the end of the book as the new regime stabilises and things return to a sort of normality for the book’s central characters; chinks of light appear in the darkness. <br /><br />The story is told in a simple, pared-down, almost fabular, style, as if trying to emulate traditional tales. Birds, animals and insects – crows, eagles, ants, locusts – assume an near-mythical role, as do trees and flowers, echoing the themes of the novel. Yet despite this, the author manages to create characters who are believable, and by setting their actions and motivations against the background of real and dramatic political events, creates real emotional drama. The narrative is particularly interesting as an insider’s rich account of the day-to-day lives of individuals whose culture is mysteriously different from that of most western readers, yet whose behaviour and emotional responses – from cowardice, to redemption, to the desire for revenge, to sapping grief – are recognisable and familiar to us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2727298304792953876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Karen Triggs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6796093-karen-triggs"><img alt="Karen Triggs" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen Triggs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen Triggs" href="/user/show/6796093-karen-triggs">Karen Triggs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847672407" href="/book/show/6688712-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6796093-karen-triggs?shelf=postcolonial">postcolonial</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6796093-karen-triggs?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer251278271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6758860112003569691">It's Iran in the `60s and `70s. Under the Shah, progressive Tehran ladies have abandoned the chador for loose headscarves which show their hair. <br/><br/>But in a small provincial town near Qom, Iran's centre of Shi'a scholarship, where several generations of carpet merchant Aqa Jaan's family live together in `the house of the mosque' of the book's title, the march of progress is less advanced. <br/><br/>When rakish Uncle Nosrat, a photographer and film-maker, visits Aqa Jaan and the others, bringing with him a tr</span>
  <span id="freeText6758860112003569691" style="display:none">It's Iran in the `60s and `70s. Under the Shah, progressive Tehran ladies have abandoned the chador for loose headscarves which show their hair. <br /><br />But in a small provincial town near Qom, Iran's centre of Shi'a scholarship, where several generations of carpet merchant Aqa Jaan's family live together in `the house of the mosque' of the book's title, the march of progress is less advanced. <br /><br />When rakish Uncle Nosrat, a photographer and film-maker, visits Aqa Jaan and the others, bringing with him a trendy girlfriend wearing sheer nylon tights, the two grandmothers who run the household are outraged. <br /><br />Luckily for the girlfriend, Aqa Jaan is a moderate (and a diplomat). He instructs the grandmothers to offer her a beautiful chador and a pair of thicker stockings as a gift, so that she can take a trip into town without setting tongues wagging. This is the politics of change one pair of tights at a time. <br /><br />Unfortunately it's not an approach which serves Aqa Jaan well as the Shah teeters and radical Shi-ite leader Ayatollah Khomeini's influence grows, because in the new Iran emerging around him there is no middle ground. <br /><br />This enthralling novel from Dutch-based Iranian émigré Khader Abdolah meshes a family saga with a dramatisation of the fall of the Shah and the subsequent takeover in 1979 by Khomeini and his henchmen. <br /><br />Family saga as political drama: it could be a clumsy device but Abdolah absolutely pulls it off, as much via the steady infusion of Islamic poetry and a sense of Islamic tradition (sounds boring, but it ain't the way he does it), as via the quality and charm of his writing. <br /><br />As anti-Shah civil unrest brews and later during the new regime's purges each member of Aqa Jaan's family is in some way implicated, for better or for worse. <br /><br />I mustn't give away the ending but look out for a liberal helping of poetic justice in the last chapter as someone gets his just desserts. <br /><br />There's not nearly enough poetic (or even regular-style) justice in world politics as a general rule. Just as well, then, that there is fiction like this, to transport us. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6758860112003569691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16697626969002779282" style="display:none">“‘Do you know what’s going to happen tonight?’<br />‘No, what?’<br />‘Two men are going to land on the moon tonight and you don’t even know it! Maybe it’s not important to you or Alsaberi. But the Americans are going to plant their flag on the moon, and the city’s imam isn’t even aware of it. He didn’t make a single reference to it in his sermon. He should’ve mentioned it tonight, but he doesn’t even know it’s happening. And that’s not good for the mosque. The mosque is where people should hear about things that affect their lives.’”<br /><br />The story discusses the house next to the mosque in Senejan and the people living inside it. It began with one family member smuggled television into the house so they can see Americans land on the moon in 1969. They felt it was important to know what’s going on in the world despite the political climate. Each character in the story adopts a different stance. Some are opposed to the American modernity imposed by the Shah and some benefit from it.<br /><br />Hossein Sadjadi Ghaemmaghami Farahani (pen name Kader Abdolah) writes in simple words that flow nicely. It is a rich mix between history and fable. There are some tales about Yunus and the fish, Solomon with the tower of ants and obviously the Iranian revolution.<br /><br />This book is one of @nrcnl best Dutch novels and was a bestseller when it was first published in 2005 which makes it more remarkable given the climate in the Netherlands during that time. Islam was increasingly sensitized following the assassination of Pim Fortuyn (2002) and Theo van Gogh (2004).</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2501802123426165349"><br/>Recieved this from librarything early reviewers last week - and had to read it straight away.<br/><br/>This is a beautifully written novel, about some of the most turbulent years in Iranian history. The book opens in 1969 at the time of the moon landings ( the back cover says 1950 - I assume that is an error). Immediately we become introduced to the family and servants of Aqa Jaan who have lived in the house of the mosque, and been custodians of the mosque itself for 8 centuries. We follow the fortunes of</span>
  <span id="freeText2501802123426165349" style="display:none"><br />Recieved this from librarything early reviewers last week - and had to read it straight away.<br /><br />This is a beautifully written novel, about some of the most turbulent years in Iranian history. The book opens in 1969 at the time of the moon landings ( the back cover says 1950 - I assume that is an error). Immediately we become introduced to the family and servants of Aqa Jaan who have lived in the house of the mosque, and been custodians of the mosque itself for 8 centuries. We follow the fortunes of the various characters as the country changes around them, the Shah deposed and the Ayatollah's take his place. During this time of upheaval loyalties are tested and often laid aside as people turn against their own, Kader Abdolah manages to convey perfectly the terror and helplessness felt by good people as so much around them turns very bad indeed. I loved the character of Aqa Jaan - a wonderfully wise and deeply human man through whose eyes we witness the tragedies the revolution brought about. A really good, hard to put down novel. Very glad I have had a chance to read it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2501802123426165349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9222691674072305615">I could not engage with this at all, and got rather annoyed with it. I found this way too schematic, dry, simplistic, and most of all: shallow. It's a critique of fundamentalism, which is good but did not go far enough for me. The characters are flat, emotions are sparsely expressed; I cannot retrace people's motivations.   <span id="freeText9222691674072305615" style="display:none">I could not engage with this at all, and got rather annoyed with it. I found this way too schematic, dry, simplistic, and most of all: shallow. It's a critique of fundamentalism, which is good but did not go far enough for me. The characters are flat, emotions are sparsely expressed; I cannot retrace people's motivations. Mysticism is weaved into the writing, which does not appeal to me at all. The writing style is just plain boring; there is no beauty in this simple language. Anyone not knowing much about Iran's history is left wondering what is true and what isn't, but as that's the case with all historic fiction, it is up to us readers to educate ourselves. I read some more on Persia/Iran, and I suppose that's the best outcome of the book for me. <br /><br /></span>

      </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847679284" href="/book/show/12444260-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6380473-anne?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6380473-anne?shelf=mena-2018">mena-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6380473-anne?shelf=family-sagas">family-sagas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2401344783" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17089137642637608112">I enjoyed this so much that I stayed up half the night reading because I couldn't put it down. Iran is a country much in the news but which I know little about. The book follows a family from the 1950s up until just after the death of Ayatollah Khomeini and the effect that changing times have on them. I remember the revolution and the war with Iraq from when I was a young teenager but this bought alive how tumultuous change brutalises ordinary people and how difficult it is to keep one's humanit</span>
  <span id="freeText17089137642637608112" style="display:none">I enjoyed this so much that I stayed up half the night reading because I couldn't put it down. Iran is a country much in the news but which I know little about. The book follows a family from the 1950s up until just after the death of Ayatollah Khomeini and the effect that changing times have on them. I remember the revolution and the war with Iraq from when I was a young teenager but this bought alive how tumultuous change brutalises ordinary people and how difficult it is to keep one's humanity in difficult circumstances. </span>

<div id="review_2500240748" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2500240748" />
    <a title="Fuat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64644500-fuat"><img alt="Fuat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521558802p2/64644500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2500240748?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fuat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fuat" href="/user/show/64644500-fuat">Fuat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847679284" href="/book/show/12444260-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2500240748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6166892349449547571">"'Fakhri, there's been a revolution, not just an ordinary transfer of power. And because of that, there's been a radical change in the way people think. We're going to see things we never would have thought possible in ordinary times. Human beings are capable of the most inhuman behaviours. Look around you: everyone's changed. You can hardly recognise them any more."'<br/><br/>Aqa Jaan is a successful carpet merchant in a peaceful town of Senejan, Iran. He lives with a big family in a big house that was i</span>
  <span id="freeText6166892349449547571" style="display:none">"'Fakhri, there's been a revolution, not just an ordinary transfer of power. And because of that, there's been a radical change in the way people think. We're going to see things we never would have thought possible in ordinary times. Human beings are capable of the most inhuman behaviours. Look around you: everyone's changed. You can hardly recognise them any more."'<br /><br />Aqa Jaan is a successful carpet merchant in a peaceful town of Senejan, Iran. He lives with a big family in a big house that was integrated with a historical mosque which he has inherited for generations. As the head of the family and the respectable mosque, he was really look up to and highly respected by the whole family and the people that live in that town. But, the simple traditional life of his family was begin to shatter when the wind of revolution came to the town Senejan and change the life of the family and people around him.<br /><br />The House of the Mosque was set in the 1970's Iranian revolution. From the secular regime of Shah Mohammad Reza Palavi to the rise of Islamic Republic. Despite the heavy subject, I find the book really easy to read and absorb. I think the author deliberately wrote the book in simplest form possible. Even a lot of characters name was simplified so the reader that was unfamiliar with Iranian names won't get confused. In doing so, Kader Abdolah has created a book that was so effective in telling the story of Iranian Revolution and what happened to the people involved.<br /><br />The House of the Mosque reminded me a bit of Midnight Children by Salman Rushdie (or maybe magical realism book in general). Maybe not so much on the fantasy aspect, but in which the extended families of Aqa Jaan was strategically involved in the most important role and event of the revolution, one of his family join the leftist movement, other become one of Ayatollah Khomeini's trusted man even one of Aqa Jaan's brother help determined the future of Iranian Cinema! And almost all the important event during that time (that I know of) was included in the book.<br /><br />I found that the book is a perfect combination of playful story, traditional value and heartwarming eastern wisdom. In the end, I find myself sympathize a lot for the family of Aqa Jaan that cling so much on religious tradition (which I'm quite opposite of). I learned a lot more about history of Islam and the Iranian Revolution in a really exciting way. Definitely one of my favorite read this year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6166892349449547571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18369046461710754948">The House of the Mosque starts somehow acceptable; showing signs of magic realism, establishes itself to be a historical novel with skewed fictionalized characters, some of whom are real historical figures. By the time that story reaches 1979 revolution it starts to shift points of views repeatedly and loses the stream of storytelling that was going rather fine before that. characters' actions become unravelled and summarized; and as a reader who rather knows what has went on in those years, I f</span>
  <span id="freeText18369046461710754948" style="display:none">The House of the Mosque starts somehow acceptable; showing signs of magic realism, establishes itself to be a historical novel with skewed fictionalized characters, some of whom are real historical figures. By the time that story reaches 1979 revolution it starts to shift points of views repeatedly and loses the stream of storytelling that was going rather fine before that. characters' actions become unravelled and summarized; and as a reader who rather knows what has went on in those years, I find those fictional actions out of context and unjustified for the sake of story. It is obvious that the writer has lost his string of thoughts somewhere in the middle of the book. Even without all these problems the book would not be so much better than an average book.<br /><br />P.S. Agha Jan, the main character of the household is a familiar traditional father figure which is still vastly considered as "the perfect man" in Iranian society.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2797366251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2797366251" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32662770-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538734547p2/32662770.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2797366251?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/32662770-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847672407" href="/book/show/6688712-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32662770-barbara?shelf=iran">iran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32662770-barbara?shelf=historic-fiction">historic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32662770-barbara?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32662770-barbara?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32662770-barbara?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32662770-barbara?shelf=domestic-drama">domestic-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2797366251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6394502571337565216">The House of the Mosque is home to three brothers and their families and has been in the hands of the family for eight centuries. It’s easy to imagine that very little has changed in all that time. Aqa Jaan is the eldest brother and patriarch of the clan. His brother Imam Alsaberi is the priest at the mosque attached to the house and the final brother, a blind man known to family and townsfolk alike as ‘Muezzin’ is, as his name suggests, the muezzin of the mosque who calls the faithful to prayer</span>
  <span id="freeText6394502571337565216" style="display:none">The House of the Mosque is home to three brothers and their families and has been in the hands of the family for eight centuries. It’s easy to imagine that very little has changed in all that time. Aqa Jaan is the eldest brother and patriarch of the clan. His brother Imam Alsaberi is the priest at the mosque attached to the house and the final brother, a blind man known to family and townsfolk alike as ‘Muezzin’ is, as his name suggests, the muezzin of the mosque who calls the faithful to prayer. Each of the men has a wife and children and the extended family all live together at the House of the Mosque in a mostly stable and peaceable way. Not everyone agrees with everyone else as you’d expect in any extended family, they have their minor irritations but on the whole, it’s not a bad life.<br /><br />The tale starts with Aqa Jaan being approached by a handsome and rakish stranger, Khalkhal, who has just arrived from the holy city of Qom to seek the hand of his daughter. All instincts tell Jaan that this man is not what he seems and despite the black turban that identifies him as a direct descendant of the prophet, something doesn’t add up. Jaan wonders if the young man is really after a wife, or if he has his eye on the mosque which is already promised to Imam Alsaberi’s son, Ahmad.<br /><br />Other sons and daughters each have their own complex decisions to make; whether to stay in the town or move to the big city; whether to follow their hearts or their heads in their educational aspirations; whether to take the easy route and comply or the difficult route of rebellion. Some fall by the wayside, lured by the call of opium and unsuitable company whilst others search for a more modern way to make their mark on their country.<br /><br />'The House of the Mosque' is pitched as a bit of a ‘simple tale of everyday folk’ who just happen to be Iranian and just happen to live by the mosque at a time of historic turmoil but there’s a lot more to this tale than a simple family saga. The status quo at the beginning of the book is one in which the small town’s society runs around the twin respect for the mosque and the bazaar. Jaan as head of the bazaar and a successful carpet trader has the double kudos of trade and religion to make him a highly respected figure. There seems to be little difference in how their town functions in the 1960s and how it would have been for many centuries before. Indeed up to the point at which one of the sons proposes to bring a television into the library of the house, you could have been reading about almost any period over the previous few hundred years. Give or take a few cars and some electricity there’s not a lot of difference between the 1960s and the 1560s.<br /><br />Iran’s leader, the American-backed Shah, starts to force unwelcome changes on the country. We as outsiders might consider young women going out into the street without a headscarf or wearing ‘nylons’ as progress but in small town Iran it’s not welcome. The Shah’s secret police intrude into the lives of the family, manipulating the weakness and frailty of one member to keep the family under control. But with Khalkhal, the radical imam, around to stir up trouble and bring the message of the clerics who support Khomeini from his place in exile, it soon becomes apparent that change is inevitable.<br /><br />The march of progress is welcomed by some and rejected by others. When those cursed Americans are about to set foot on the moon, one of the sons insists that the Imam can’t ignore it and he smuggles a television into the house so the Imam and his brother can witness the historic moments. When the ‘grandmothers’ (two elderly ladies related to nobody in the family) go off to make the Hajj to Mecca, you can understand that the change of scene might go to their heads. With so much change on the horizon, who’d want to go home? Not long after, the dastardly dodgy Imam son-in-law is whipping up a frenzy against the opening of a cinema in the town. Freedoms are hard won and with the revolution, swiftly rescinded.<br /><br />Imams are shown to be a bunch with feet of clay. Poor old Alsaberi has some funny obsessive-compulsive tendencies around cleanliness which ultimately contribute to his early demise. His son-in-law, the power-hungry Khalkhal turns out to be the biggest bastard in the entire tale, on so many different levels, whilst his replacement has too much of an eye for the ladies. When Alsaberi’s son Ahmad comes back from the seminary to take his place, he’s a little too fond of the good things in life.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed 'The House of the Mosque' – if one can be said to enjoy a book in which such horrific things are recounted. Oppression and repression are rife, thousands of people are slaughtered for not toeing the line or to settle old scores. Good people turn power-crazy and torture their friends and neighbours, bad people get power beyond their wildest and craziest dreams and in the midst of this, a few get close to the man behind the slaughter, Ayatollah Khomeini.<br /><br />There are good people in this tale too. One of the sons becomes a film-maker and a close confidante of the Ayatollah without really compromising his personal and artistic integrity. He introduces the leader to the delights of cinema at great personal risk and even starts filming him and his family. Another man helps Jaan when nobody will give a burial space to a member of the family shot dead by Khomeini’s appointed terminator and the same man brings about one of the most redemptive moments of the entire book when Jaan visits him many years later.<br /><br />I intentionally avoided reading the author’s biographical notes in the back of the book because I didn’t want too much knowledge to get in the way of my reading and equally I don’t want to say too much in this review that might influence another reader. Sometimes you can know too much and it influences your perceptions of the story. I knew only that Kader Abdolah now lives in Europe and writes in Dutch. It was clear from this that he’d probably left Iran at some point but I didn’t want to know when or why until I’d finished reading. There are so many conflicting reasons why people have left in the past few decades. He could have been an opponent of the Shah, a supporter of the Shah or as an émigré fleeing the repressions of the Khomeini regime. You just can’t second guess with so many good reasons to get out. But in the end, I didn’t need to know because he writes himself into the final pages, making himself one of the characters in the family and pulling all the loose ends neatly together again.<br /><br />Abdolah has written several other novels and short stories. It’s not easy to find books set in and even more difficult to find ones that tackle the dark side of the Revolution. I’d suspect that it’s only possible to write a book like this if you’ve already burnt your bridges and left the country or if you have a cast iron alibi. However, this fascinating country and its recent history need to be brought to the attention of more people through books like 'The House of the Mosque'. Abdolah’s a brave man to lift the lid on post-Revolutionary horrors and I hope he doesn’t have to live his life looking over his shoulder.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6394502571337565216" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94759946" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12827427795665280874">‘There was once a house, an old house which was known as the house of the mosque.’<br/><br/>In the house of the mosque, located in Senejan, Iran, the family of Aqa Jaan has lived for eight centuries. The house is currently occupied by the families of Aqa Jaan, a merchant who is the head of the city’s bazaar; Alsaberi; the imam of the mosque and Aqa Shoja, the mosque’s muezzin. The carpets woven by the family firm are renowned for their beauty, their patterns are drawn from the plumage of birds Aqa Jaan’s </span>
  <span id="freeText12827427795665280874" style="display:none">‘There was once a house, an old house which was known as the house of the mosque.’<br /><br />In the house of the mosque, located in Senejan, Iran, the family of Aqa Jaan has lived for eight centuries. The house is currently occupied by the families of Aqa Jaan, a merchant who is the head of the city’s bazaar; Alsaberi; the imam of the mosque and Aqa Shoja, the mosque’s muezzin. The carpets woven by the family firm are renowned for their beauty, their patterns are drawn from the plumage of birds Aqa Jaan’s wife traps on the roof of the house. This is the order of things in Senejan, in 1969: a rich past continuing into the future. The grandmothers sweep the floors each morning, and dream of travelling to Mecca. Sadiq is waiting for a suitor to knock on the door to seek her hand in marriage. In the first half of this novel, the worlds of Aqa Jaan and his family members are neatly ordered. <br /> <br />Except, things start changing. Aqa Jaan’s nephew Shahbal, with permission, smuggles a television into the house so that Aqa Jaan and the imam can watch the moon landing. The nephew argues that the imam needs to keep in touch with the world, even if those landing on the moon are the Americans, and a television is part of the suspect civilization that the Shah is imposing on his people. <br /><br />In the second half of the novel, the consequences of political unrest in Iran, both before and after the revolution of 1978-79 are being felt. Small changes at first, but then the fall of the Shah and the return of the Ayatollah destroy the established order of the house of the mosque. The world turns upside down: Shahbal backs the Islamic revolution, while Aqa Jaan’s other nephew, Nosrat, a westernised film-maker, becomes a member of Khomeini’s inner circle. Nothing seems predictable.<br /><br />This is a complex novel, but not difficult to read. Kader Abdolah creates a multi-layered world: filled with interesting characters living, and sometimes losing, their lives in the turmoil of revolutionary change. And Aqa Jaan himself, and the house of the mosque, are not unchanged. What does the future hold?<br /><br />‘Our story is over, but the crow still hasn’t reached its nest.’<br /><br />Jennifer Cameron-Smith<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9473830773504477054">3.5 stars. <br/>Okay, this was a really hard book to grade. <br/>First of all, I thought the first half of the book was quite boring. So boring that I stopped reading the book about 2 years ago. I then decided to finish it.<br/>When it was coming to an end it started to be more interesting with many more exciting events. <br/>I hated the fact that the author kept using wordings like "Allah's judges" without some sort of quotations showing that thats only what they called them and that they aren't really Allah's jud</span>
  <span id="freeText9473830773504477054" style="display:none">3.5 stars. <br />Okay, this was a really hard book to grade. <br />First of all, I thought the first half of the book was quite boring. So boring that I stopped reading the book about 2 years ago. I then decided to finish it.<br />When it was coming to an end it started to be more interesting with many more exciting events. <br />I hated the fact that the author kept using wordings like "Allah's judges" without some sort of quotations showing that thats only what they called them and that they aren't really Allah's judges in Islam. I also disliked the fact that he did't stress upon how Khomeini's regime was fanatical and even the rebels or Aqa Jan himself is a Shiia Muslim and that they have a couple of weird and wrong ideologies about Islam. Maybe I should've accepted this point of view more, nonetheless; it made me grateful that I am a Sunni Muslim. <br />I also had some issue of him not sourcing the Quranic verses properly, like which chapters they're are from, most of the time(so people who don't know its Quran would get confused if there were poems written in the same italic way). The Quran is sacred and should be treated with care and well understanding. Sometimes using the verses randomly, I think wasn't a good idea, though i liked how he ended it with verses of the Quran from the Noor Chapter. So his religious aspect of this book wasn't the best in my opinion though, I liked how by the end, he had a very rich historical part in the book. It really made me understand, and also be able to relate with my countries dictator government (Abdel Fattah El SiSi, in Egypt) who is currently using the same, horrible tactics used by Khomeini.</span>

<div id="review_1093776162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1093776162" />
    <a title="Marie Østvold" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15381319-marie-stvold"><img alt="Marie Østvold" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548353361p2/15381319.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1093776162?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marie Østvold" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marie Østvold" href="/user/show/15381319-marie-stvold">Marie Østvold</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15381319-marie-stvold?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15381319-marie-stvold?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15381319-marie-stvold?shelf=2000s">2000s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1093776162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16950341536355951757">I have always been interested in Persian history and culture, so this novel caught my interest right away. An intimate story about a family in a small town, and their tradition and everyday life at the bazar and in the mosque. Their relationship to religion, state, old traditions and culture, and to modernity- television, radio, and the landing on the moon. This is unfolds with the characters of Galgal/Zinat, and Sahbal/Nosrat- and my favorite Kazem Kahn, the old poet. Also, it's a macro perspec</span>
  <span id="freeText16950341536355951757" style="display:none">I have always been interested in Persian history and culture, so this novel caught my interest right away. An intimate story about a family in a small town, and their tradition and everyday life at the bazar and in the mosque. Their relationship to religion, state, old traditions and culture, and to modernity- television, radio, and the landing on the moon. This is unfolds with the characters of Galgal/Zinat, and Sahbal/Nosrat- and my favorite Kazem Kahn, the old poet. Also, it's a macro perspective of the changes in Iran during the 70's from the Iraq war and the rise of Khomeini. <br /><br />I have to admit, I almost read it as a history book- mainly because I have an interest in the Middle-East, and especially Iran. I suppose a novel should never really be read as a history book, but it happened for me anyway. It is also at times as intese as a thriller. But what makes this book such a beautiful piece of work, lies in the descriptions; and Abdolah has a way with beautiful words. At least I was carried away by them. In particular I apreciated the ending chapter- what a garden of happiness, love, warmth and hope. I was easily swept away to the village where the birds was singing and hear the speech of the crow itting underneath the almond tree when the sun sets. I could hear the minarets and smell the safran. Now, who can give me a persian carpet?</span>
  <a data-text-id="16950341536355951757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1486696354" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5473978867566569683">"... there's been a revolution, not just an ordinary transition of power. And because of that, there's been a radical change in the way people think. We're going to see things we never would have thought possible in ordinary times. Human beings are capable of the most inhuman behaviour. Look around you; everyone's changed. You can hardly recognise them any more. I can't tell if they've suddenly dropped their masks or put on new ones."<br/><br/>This book broke my heart. It's such a beautiful depiction of o</span>
  <span id="freeText5473978867566569683" style="display:none">"... there's been a revolution, not just an ordinary transition of power. And because of that, there's been a radical change in the way people think. We're going to see things we never would have thought possible in ordinary times. Human beings are capable of the most inhuman behaviour. Look around you; everyone's changed. You can hardly recognise them any more. I can't tell if they've suddenly dropped their masks or put on new ones."<br /><br />This book broke my heart. It's such a beautiful depiction of ordinary people living in interesting times and what that can do to them. Poetry and religion are perfectly interwoven with a casual observation on how different people will adapt to crisis. Pillars of the community become broken husks, unremarkable persons become horrific monsters, and all throughout we are reminded that the only thing that can be relied upon in our darkest moments is our faith. Be it in art, family, the Divine, or simply in the concept of love, faith can make you immortal.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1920891508929411689">I highly recommend reading fiction to understand history. This is the story of a household in Iran and the changes that they went through from when the Shah was in power through the revolution and years of Ayatollah Khomeini. It was horrifying to read the effect of these changes. The Shah was a secular leader who was overthrown by fundamentalist Islamists who imposed strict Sharia law as they interpreted it. The families living in the House of the Mosque (it's a house attached to a mosque) were </span>
  <span id="freeText1920891508929411689" style="display:none">I highly recommend reading fiction to understand history. This is the story of a household in Iran and the changes that they went through from when the Shah was in power through the revolution and years of Ayatollah Khomeini. It was horrifying to read the effect of these changes. The Shah was a secular leader who was overthrown by fundamentalist Islamists who imposed strict Sharia law as they interpreted it. The families living in the House of the Mosque (it's a house attached to a mosque) were moderate Muslims, strong believers and followers of the Koran but the changes, both internal and external forces, affected the families living there, tearing apart their lives with only a few survivors. </span>

<div id="review_1212212266" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1212212266" />
    <a title="Rizky Suleman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7051018-rizky-suleman"><img alt="Rizky Suleman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450105958p2/7051018.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1212212266?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rizky Suleman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rizky Suleman" href="/user/show/7051018-rizky-suleman">Rizky Suleman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847672407" href="/book/show/6688712-the-house-of-the-mosque">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who wants to learns about Iran, Shia and Persian Carpets</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7051018-rizky-suleman?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7051018-rizky-suleman?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7051018-rizky-suleman?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1212212266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1600569222728199166">this book is so much fun to read. at first chapters this book will make you laugh a lot. for those who have no idea about Iran and Shia this book give a glimpse about their daily life, traditions even politics. the setting of this novel is just on the verge of the Islamic Revolution until the calming periods afterwards. this book teaches about firmness on principles and how important a family is. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LG0FxhQowZtM7A8zPuXYqXMinTC7607Mh8eYxBw6kjQi19QhQZeGl2nTmiZrFNy4JN2n6NMWtixAZREoW7IcQA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1086389'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1086389.Joe_Speedboot?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Joe Speedboot<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/537632.Tommy_Wieringa?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Tommy Wieringa<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.71 avg rating &mdash; 10,193 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2005\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer15920122492325669669\">Joe Speedboot is een roman over liefde en rivaliteit, over een verlosser zonder belofte en een heroïsche odyssee. Het toont het lot van nieuwkomers in een Nederlands dorp, waar de last van het onverwerkte verleden op de inwoners drukt. De tragikomisc<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15920122492325669669\" style=\"display:none\">Joe Speedboot is een roman over liefde en rivaliteit, over een verlosser zonder belofte en een heroïsche odyssee. Het toont het lot van nieuwkomers in een Nederlands dorp, waar de last van het onverwerkte verleden op de inwoners drukt. De tragikomische maalstroom van gebeurtenissen geeft het ingeslapen dorp een onverwachte dynamiek waar het niet meer van zal herstellen.Tommy Wieringa schreef met Joe Speedboot een gedreven, aanstekelijke ontwikkelingsroman, met een hoofdrol voor de mateloze verbeeldingskracht van één man.Fransje Hermans, de verteller van het verhaal, is na een ongeluk invalide geraakt en heeft voorgoed zijn spraakvermogen verloren. Hij is de zelfbenoemde chroniqueur van het dorp Lomark. Zijn fascinatie voor de nieuweling Joe Speedboot is grenzeloos: Joe Speedboot, de jongen die zijn eigen naam gekozen heeft en op zijn vijftiende al bommenlegger, vliegtuigbouwer en bewegingsfilosoof is. Nauwgezet observeert Fransje hoe nog een nieuwkomer de natuurlijke orde van het dorp komt verstoren: Joe\'s stiefvader Papa Afrika, een zachtaardige Nubiër met gazellenogen die een kleine scheepswerf begint op de oever van de Rijn. Dan verschijnt de geheimzinnige Picolien Jane, een beeldschone Zuid-Afrikaanse, aan wie Fransje zijn kronieken opdraagt en voor wie levenslange vriendschappen op het spel worden gezet. In haar komen alle verhalen samen, met noodlottige gevolgen.Op originele wijze en met speels vernuft, toont Tommy Wieringa de loop van een geschiedenis, waarin niemand aan zijn bestemming ontkomt.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15920122492325669669\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer574843364425836922">Kader Abdolah is the penname of Hossein Sadjadi Ghaemmaghami Farahani, an Iranian writer who also writes in Dutch. Abdolah has lived in the Netherlands since 1988.<br/><br/>He studied physics at the Arak College of Science and fled the country as a political refugee in 1988. Today he lives in Delft (The Netherlands), writing under a pseudonym made up of the names of two murdered friends. <i>Het huis van de mos</i></span>
  <span id="freeText574843364425836922" style="display:none">Kader Abdolah is the penname of Hossein Sadjadi Ghaemmaghami Farahani, an Iranian writer who also writes in Dutch. Abdolah has lived in the Netherlands since 1988.<br /><br />He studied physics at the Arak College of Science and fled the country as a political refugee in 1988. Today he lives in Delft (The Netherlands), writing under a pseudonym made up of the names of two murdered friends. <i>Het huis van de moskee</i> (<i>The House of the Mosque</i>), catapulted Abdolah into the Dutch bestseller lists.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="574843364425836922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
